	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Joseph Schumpeter</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Austrian political economist</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Austrian economist</div>

<p><b>Joseph Aloïs Schumpeter</b> (<small>German:&#32;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈʃʊmpeːtɐ]</a></span>; 8 February 1883 – 8 January 1950)<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> was an Austrian <a href="/wiki/Political_economy" title="Political economy">political economist</a>. Born in Moravia, he briefly served as <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Finance_(Austria)" title="Ministry of Finance (Austria)">Finance Minister of Austria</a> in 1919. In 1932, he became a professor at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> where he remained until the end of his career, eventually obtaining U.S. citizenship.
</p><p>One of the most influential <a href="/wiki/Economist" title="Economist">economists</a> of the 20th century, Schumpeter popularized the term "<a href="/wiki/Creative_destruction" title="Creative destruction">creative destruction</a>" in <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Schumpeter was born in Triesch, <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Moravia" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburg Moravia">Habsburg Moravia</a> (now <a href="/wiki/T%C5%99e%C5%A1%C5%A5" title="Třešť">Třešť</a> in the Czech Republic, then part of <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>) in 1883 to <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/German-speaking" class="mw-redirect" title="German-speaking">German-speaking</a> parents. Both of his grandmothers were <a href="/wiki/Czechs" title="Czechs">Czech</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-origin1_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-origin1-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Schumpeter did not acknowledge his Czech ancestry; he considered himself an <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_German" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic German">ethnic German</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-origin1_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-origin1-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> His father owned a factory, but he died when Joseph was only four years old.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In 1893, Joseph and his mother moved to <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Schumpeter was a loyal supporter of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Joseph_I_of_Austria" title="Franz Joseph I of Austria">Franz Joseph I of Austria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-origin1_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-origin1-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After attending school at the <a href="/wiki/Theresianum" title="Theresianum">Theresianum</a>, Schumpeter began his career studying law at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Vienna" title="University of Vienna">University of Vienna</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_School" title="Austrian School">Austrian</a> capital theorist <a href="/wiki/Eugen_von_B%C3%B6hm-Bawerk" class="mw-redirect" title="Eugen von Böhm-Bawerk">Eugen von Böhm-Bawerk</a>, taking his <a href="/wiki/PhD" class="mw-redirect" title="PhD">PhD</a> in 1906. In 1909, after some study trips, he became a professor of economics and government at the <a href="/wiki/Chernivtsi_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Chernivtsi University">University of Czernowitz</a>. In 1911, he joined the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Graz" title="University of Graz">University of Graz</a>, where he remained until <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.
</p><p>In 1918, Schumpeter was a member of the Socialization Commission established by the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_People%27s_Deputies" title="Council of the People&#39;s Deputies">Council of the People's Deputies</a> in Germany. In March 1919, he was invited to take office as Minister of Finance in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_German-Austria" title="Republic of German-Austria">Republic of German-Austria</a>. He proposed a capital levy as a way to tackle the war debt and opposed the socialization of the Alpine Mountain plant.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 1921, he became president of the private Biedermann Bank. He was also a board member at the Kaufmann Bank. Problems at those banks left Schumpeter in debt. His resignation was a condition of the takeover of the Biedermann Bank in September 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1925 to 1932, Schumpeter held a chair at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bonn" title="University of Bonn">University of Bonn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>. He lectured at <a href="/wiki/Harvard" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard">Harvard</a> in 1927–1928 and 1930. In 1931, he was a visiting professor at <a href="/wiki/The_Tokyo_College_of_Commerce" class="mw-redirect" title="The Tokyo College of Commerce">The Tokyo College of Commerce</a>. In 1932, Schumpeter moved to the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, and soon began what would become extensive efforts to help central European economist colleagues displaced by <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Schumpeter also became known for his opposition to <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a> and socialism that he thought would lead to dictatorship, and even criticized President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin Roosevelt's</a> <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In 1939, Schumpeter became a US citizen. In the beginning of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the <a href="/wiki/FBI" class="mw-redirect" title="FBI">FBI</a> investigated him and his wife, Elizabeth Boody (a prominent scholar of Japanese economics) for pro-Nazi leanings, but found no evidence of Nazi sympathies.<sup id="cite_ref-Entrepreneurship_2007,_p._5_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Entrepreneurship_2007,_p._5-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At Harvard, Schumpeter was considered a memorable character, erudite and even showy in the classroom. He became known for his heavy teaching load and his personal and painstaking interest in his students.  He served as the faculty advisor of the Graduate Economics Club and organized private seminars and discussion groups.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Some colleagues thought his views outdated by <a href="/wiki/Keynesianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Keynesianism">Keynesianism</a> which was fashionable; others resented his criticisms, particularly of their failure to offer an assistant professorship to <a href="/wiki/Paul_Samuelson" title="Paul Samuelson">Paul Samuelson</a>, but recanted when they thought him likely to accept a position at <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This period of his life was characterized by hard work and comparatively little recognition of his massive 2-volume book <i>Business Cycles.</i> However, the Schumpeters persevered, and in 1942 published what became the most popular of all his works, <i><a href="/wiki/Capitalism,_Socialism_and_Democracy" title="Capitalism, Socialism and Democracy">Capitalism, Socialism and Democracy</a></i>, reprinted many times and in many languages in the following decades, as well as cited thousands of times.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The source of Joseph Schumpeter's dynamic, change-oriented, and innovation-based economics was the <a href="/wiki/Historical_school_of_economics" title="Historical school of economics">Historical School of economics</a>. Although his writings could be critical of the School, Schumpeter's work on the role of innovation and <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurship" title="Entrepreneurship">entrepreneurship</a> can be seen as a continuation of ideas originated by the Historical School, especially the work of <a href="/wiki/Gustav_von_Schmoller" title="Gustav von Schmoller">Gustav von Schmoller</a> and <a href="/wiki/Werner_Sombart" title="Werner Sombart">Werner Sombart</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_economics">Evolutionary economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Evolutionary economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_economics" title="Evolutionary economics">Evolutionary economics</a></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Freeman" title="Christopher Freeman">Christopher Freeman</a> (2009), a scholar who devoted much time researching Schumpeter's work: "the central point of his whole life work [is]: that capitalism can only be understood as an evolutionary process of continuous innovation and '<a href="/wiki/Creative_destruction" title="Creative destruction">creative destruction</a>'".<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_Economic_Analysis"><i>History of Economic Analysis</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History of Economic Analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Schumpeter's scholarship is apparent in his posthumous <i>History of Economic Analysis</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> although some of his judgments seem <a href="/wiki/Idiosyncratic" class="mw-redirect" title="Idiosyncratic">idiosyncratic</a> and sometimes cavalier. For instance, Schumpeter thought that the greatest 18th century economist was <a href="/wiki/Anne_Robert_Jacques_Turgot,_Baron_de_Laune" class="mw-redirect" title="Anne Robert Jacques Turgot, Baron de Laune">Turgot</a>, not <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>, as many consider, and he considered <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Walras" title="Léon Walras">Léon Walras</a> to be the "greatest of all economists", beside whom other economists' theories were "like inadequate attempts to catch some particular aspects of Walrasian truth".<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>  Schumpeter criticized <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Ricardo" title="David Ricardo">David Ricardo</a> for the "Ricardian vice." According to Schumpeter, Ricardo and Keynes reasoned in terms of abstract models, where they would freeze all but a few variables. Then they could argue that one caused the other in a simple <a href="/wiki/Monotonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monotonic">monotonic</a> fashion. This led to the belief that one could easily deduce policy conclusions directly from a highly abstract theoretical model.
</p><p>In this book, Joseph Schumpeter recognized the implication of a <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold monetary standard</a> compared to a <a href="/wiki/Fiat_currency" class="mw-redirect" title="Fiat currency">fiat monetary standard</a>. In <i>History of Economic Analysis</i>, Schumpeter stated the following: "An 'automatic' gold currency is part and parcel of a <a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free-trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Free-trade">free-trade</a> economy. It links every nation's money rates and price levels with the money-rates and price levels of all the other nations that are 'on gold.' However, gold is extremely sensitive to government expenditure and even to attitudes or policies that do not involve expenditure directly, for example, to foreign policy, to certain policies of taxation, and, in general, to precisely all those policies that violate the principles of [classical] liberalism. <i>This</i> is the reason why gold is so unpopular now and also why it was so popular in a <a href="/wiki/Bourgeois" class="mw-redirect" title="Bourgeois">bourgeois</a> era."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_cycles">Business cycles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Business cycles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Schumpeter's relationships with the ideas of other economists were quite complex in his most important contributions to economic analysis – the theory of <a href="/wiki/Business_cycle" title="Business cycle">business cycles</a> and development. Following neither Walras nor Keynes, Schumpeter starts in <i>The Theory of Economic Development<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></i> with a treatise of <a href="/wiki/Circular_flow" class="mw-redirect" title="Circular flow">circular flow</a> which, excluding any innovations and innovative activities, leads to a stationary state. The stationary state is, according to Schumpeter, described by <a href="/wiki/Walrasian_equilibrium" class="mw-redirect" title="Walrasian equilibrium">Walrasian equilibrium</a>. The hero of his story is the <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneur" class="mw-redirect" title="Entrepreneur">entrepreneur</a>.
</p><p>The entrepreneur disturbs this equilibrium and is the prime cause of economic development, which proceeds in cyclic fashion along several time scales. In fashioning this theory connecting innovations, cycles, and development, Schumpeter kept alive the Russian <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Kondratiev" title="Nikolai Kondratiev">Nikolai Kondratiev</a>'s ideas on 50-year cycles, <a href="/wiki/Kondratiev_wave" title="Kondratiev wave">Kondratiev waves</a>.
</p><p>Schumpeter suggested a model in which the four main cycles, <a href="/wiki/Kondratiev_wave" title="Kondratiev wave">Kondratiev</a> (54 years), Kuznets (18 years), <a href="/wiki/Clement_Juglar" class="mw-redirect" title="Clement Juglar">Juglar</a> (9 years) and <a href="/wiki/Kitchin_cycle" title="Kitchin cycle">Kitchin</a> (about 4 years) can be added together to form a composite <a href="/wiki/Waveform" title="Waveform">waveform</a>. Actually there was considerable professional rivalry between Schumpeter and Kuznets. The wave form suggested here did not include the <a href="/wiki/Kuznets_swing" title="Kuznets swing">Kuznets Cycle</a> simply because Schumpeter did not recognize it as a valid cycle.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2009)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> See "<a href="/wiki/Business_cycle" title="Business cycle">business cycle</a>" for further information. A Kondratiev wave could consist of three lower degree Kuznets waves.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Each Kuznets wave could, itself, be made up of two Juglar waves. Similarly two (or three) Kitchin waves could form a higher degree Juglar wave. If each of these were in phase, more importantly if the downward arc of each was simultaneous so that the <a href="/wiki/Nadir" title="Nadir">nadir</a> of each was coincident, it would explain disastrous slumps and consequent depressions. As far as the segmentation of the Kondratiev Wave, Schumpeter never proposed such a fixed model. He saw these cycles varying in time – although in  a tight time frame by coincidence – and for each to serve a specific purpose.
</p>
<table class="toccolours" style="float:right; margin: 0.5em 0 1.3em 1.4em;" border="0">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Keynesianism">Keynesianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Keynesianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Schumpeter's theory, <a href="/wiki/Walrasian_equilibrium" class="mw-redirect" title="Walrasian equilibrium">Walrasian equilibrium</a> is not adequate to capture the key mechanisms of economic development. Schumpeter also thought that the institution enabling the entrepreneur to buy the resources needed to realize his vision was a well-developed <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalist</a> financial system, including a whole range of institutions for granting <a href="/wiki/Credit_(education)" class="mw-redirect" title="Credit (education)">credit</a>. One could divide economists among (1) those who emphasized "real" analysis and regarded money as merely a "veil" and (2) those who thought monetary institutions are important and money could be a separate driving force. Both Schumpeter and Keynes were among the latter.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demise_of_capitalism">Demise of capitalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Demise of capitalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Joseph+Schumpeter%22">"Joseph Schumpeter"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Joseph+Schumpeter%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Joseph+Schumpeter%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Joseph+Schumpeter%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Joseph+Schumpeter%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Joseph+Schumpeter%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Schumpeter's most popular book in English is probably <i><a href="/wiki/Capitalism,_Socialism_and_Democracy" title="Capitalism, Socialism and Democracy">Capitalism, Socialism and Democracy</a></i>. While he agrees with <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> that capitalism will collapse and be replaced by <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>, Schumpeter predicts a different way this will come about. While Marx predicted that capitalism would be overthrown by a violent proletarian revolution, which actually occurred in the least capitalist countries, Schumpeter believed that capitalism would gradually weaken by itself and eventually collapse. Specifically, the success of capitalism would lead to <a href="/wiki/Corporatism" title="Corporatism">corporatism</a> and to values hostile to capitalism, especially among <a href="/wiki/Intellectual" title="Intellectual">intellectuals</a>.
</p><p>"Intellectuals" are a social class in a position to critique societal matters for which they are not directly responsible and to stand up for the interests of other classes. Intellectuals tend to have a negative outlook of capitalism, even while relying on it for prestige, because their professions rely on antagonism toward it. The growing number of people with higher education is a great advantage of capitalism, according to Schumpeter. Yet, unemployment and a lack of fulfilling work will cause intellectual critique, discontent and protests.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">Parliaments</a> will increasingly elect <a href="/wiki/Social_democratic" class="mw-redirect" title="Social democratic">social democratic</a> parties, and democratic majorities will vote for restrictions on entrepreneurship. Increasing <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_self-management" title="Workers&#39; self-management">workers' self-management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Industrial_democracy" title="Industrial democracy">industrial democracy</a> and regulatory institutions would evolve non-politically into "<a href="/wiki/Liberal_capitalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal capitalism">liberal capitalism</a>". Thus, the intellectual and social climate needed for thriving <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurship" title="Entrepreneurship">entrepreneurship</a> will be replaced by some form of "<a href="/wiki/Laborism" class="mw-redirect" title="Laborism">laborism</a>". This will exacerbate "<a href="/wiki/Creative_destruction" title="Creative destruction">creative destruction</a>" (a borrowed phrase to denote an <a href="/wiki/Endogeneity_(economics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Endogeneity (economics)">endogenous</a> replacement of old ways of doing things by new ways), which will ultimately undermine and destroy the capitalist structure.
</p><p>Schumpeter emphasizes throughout this book that he is analyzing trends, not engaging in political advocacy.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>William Fellner, in the book <i>Schumpeter’s Vision: Capitalism, Socialism and Democracy After 40 Years</i>, noted that Schumpeter saw any political system in which the power was fully monopolized as fascist.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratic_theory">Democratic theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Democratic theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the same book, Schumpeter expounded a theory of <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a> which sought to challenge what he called the "classical doctrine". He disputed the idea that democracy was a process by which the electorate identified the common good, and politicians carried this out for them. He argued this was unrealistic, and that people's ignorance and superficiality meant that in fact they were largely manipulated by politicians, who set the agenda. Furthermore, he claimed that even if the common good was possible to find, it would still not make clear the means needed to reach its end, since citizens do not have the requisite knowledge to design government policy.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> This made a 'rule by the people' concept both unlikely and undesirable. Instead he advocated a minimalist model, much influenced by <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a>, whereby democracy is the mechanism for competition between leaders, much like a market structure. Although periodic votes by the general public legitimize governments and keep them accountable, the policy program is very much seen as their own and not that of the people, and the participatory role for individuals is usually severely limited.
</p><p>Schumpeter defined democracy as the method by which people elected representatives in competitive elections to carry out their will.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> This definition has been described as simple, elegant and parsimonious, making it clearer to distinguish political systems that either fulfill or fail these characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This minimalist definition stands in contrast to broader definitions of democracy, which may emphasize aspects such as "representation, accountability, equality, participation, justice, dignity, rationality, security, freedom."<sup id="cite_ref-:0_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Within such a minimalist definition, states which other scholars say have experienced democratic backsliding and which lack civil liberties, a free press, the rule of law and a constrained executive, would still be considered democracies.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> For Schumpeter, the formation of a government is the endpoint of the democratic process, which means that for the purposes of his democratic theory, he has no comment on what kinds of decisions that the government can take in order to be a democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Schumpeter faced pushback on his theory from other democratic theorists, such as Robert Dahl, who argued that there is more to democracy than simply the formation of government through competitive elections.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Schumpeter's view of democracy has been described as "elitist", as he criticizes the rationality and knowledge of voters, and expresses a preference for politicians taking decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Democracy is therefore in a sense a means to ensure circulation among elites.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> However, studies by Natasha Piano (of the University of Chicago) emphasize that Schumpeter had substantial disdain for elites.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entrepreneurship">Entrepreneurship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Entrepreneurship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Schumpeter was probably the first scholar to theorize about <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurship" title="Entrepreneurship">entrepreneurship</a>, and the field owed much to his contributions. His fundamental theories are often referred to<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> as Mark I and Mark II. In Mark I, Schumpeter argued that the innovation and technological change of a nation come from the entrepreneurs, or wild spirits. He coined the word <i>Unternehmergeist</i>, German for "entrepreneur-spirit", and asserted that "... the doing of new things or the doing of things that are already being done in a new way"<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> stemmed directly from the efforts of entrepreneurs.
</p><p>Schumpeter developed Mark II while a professor at <a href="/wiki/Harvard" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard">Harvard</a>. Many social economists and popular authors of the day argued that large businesses had a negative effect on the standard of living of ordinary people. Contrary to this prevailing opinion, Schumpeter argued that the agents that drive innovation and the economy are large companies which have the capital to invest in <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">research and development</a> of new products and services and to deliver them to customers more cheaply, thus raising their standard of living. In one of his seminal works, <i>Capitalism, Socialism and Democracy</i>, Schumpeter wrote:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>As soon as we go into details and inquire into the individual items in which progress was most conspicuous, the trail leads not to the doors of those firms that work under conditions of comparatively free competition but precisely to the door of the large concerns – which, as in the case of agricultural machinery, also account for much of the progress in the competitive sector – and a shocking suspicion dawns upon us that big business may have had more to do with creating that standard of life than with keeping it down.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> Mark I and Mark II arguments are considered complementary.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cycles_and_long_wave_theory">Cycles and long wave theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cycles and long wave theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Schumpeter was the most influential thinker to argue that long cycles are caused by innovation, and are an incident of it. His treatise on business cycles developed were based on Kondratiev's ideas which attributed the causes very differently. Schumpeter's treatise brought Kondratiev's ideas to the attention of English-speaking economists. Kondratiev fused important elements that Schumpeter missed. Yet, the Schumpeterian variant of long-cycles hypothesis, stressing the initiating role of innovations, commands the widest attention today.
<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
In Schumpeter's view, technological innovation is at the cause of both cyclical instability and economic growth. Fluctuations in innovation cause fluctuation in investment and those cause cycles in economic growth. Schumpeter sees innovations as clustering around certain points in time periods that he refers to as "neighborhoods of equilibrium", when entrepreneurs perceive that risk and returns warrant innovative commitments. These clusters lead to long cycles by generating periods of acceleration in aggregate growth.
<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The technological view of change needs to demonstrate that changes in the rate of innovation governs changes in the rate of new investments, and that the combined impact of innovation clusters takes the form of fluctuation in aggregate output or employment. The process of technological innovation involves extremely complex relations among a set of key variables: inventions, innovations, diffusion paths and investment activities. The impact of technological innovation on aggregate output is mediated through a succession of relationships that have yet to be explored systematically in the context of long wave. New inventions are typically primitive, their performance is usually poorer than existing technologies and the cost of their production is high. A production technology may not yet exist, as is often the case in major chemical inventions, pharmaceutical inventions. The speed with which inventions are transformed into innovations and diffused depends on actual and expected trajectory of performance improvement and cost reduction.
<sup id="cite_ref-jstor.org_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Innovation">Innovation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Innovation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Schumpeter identified innovation as the critical dimension of economic change.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> He argued that economic change revolves around innovation, entrepreneurial activities, and market power. He sought to prove that innovation-originated market power can provide better results than the invisible hand and price competition. He argued that technological innovation often creates temporary monopolies, allowing abnormal profits that would soon be competed away by rivals and imitators. These temporary monopolies were necessary to provide the incentive for firms to develop new products and processes.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>He was married three times.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> His first wife was Gladys Ricarde Seaver, an Englishwoman nearly 12 years his senior (married 1907, separated 1913, divorced 1925). His best man at his wedding was his friend and Austrian jurist <a href="/wiki/Hans_Kelsen" title="Hans Kelsen">Hans Kelsen</a>. His second was Anna Reisinger, 20 years his junior and daughter of the concierge of the apartment where he grew up. As a divorced man, he and his bride converted to <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> in order to marry.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> They married in 1925, but within a year, she died in childbirth. The loss of his wife and newborn son came only weeks after Schumpeter's mother had died. In 1937, Schumpeter married the American economic historian Elizabeth Boody, who helped him popularize his work and edited what became their magnum opus, the posthumously published <i>History of Economic Analysis</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Schumpeter claimed that he had set himself three goals in life: to be the greatest economist in the world, to be the best horseman in all of <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and the greatest lover in all of <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>. He said he had reached two of his goals, but he never said which two,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> although he is reported to have said that there were too many fine horsemen in Austria for him to succeed in all his aspirations.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life_and_death">Later life and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Later life and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Schumpeter died in his home in <a href="/wiki/Taconic,_Connecticut" class="mw-redirect" title="Taconic, Connecticut">Taconic</a>, Connecticut, at the age of 66, on the night of 7 January 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For some time after his death, Schumpeter's views were most influential among various <a href="/wiki/Heterodox_economics" title="Heterodox economics">heterodox economists</a>, especially European, who were interested in industrial organization, <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary theory</a>, and economic development, and who tended to be on the other end of the political spectrum from Schumpeter and were  also often influenced by Keynes, Karl Marx, and <a href="/wiki/Thorstein_Veblen" title="Thorstein Veblen">Thorstein Veblen</a>. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Heilbroner" title="Robert Heilbroner">Robert Heilbroner</a> was one of Schumpeter's most renowned pupils, who wrote extensively about him in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Worldly_Philosophers" class="mw-redirect" title="The Worldly Philosophers">The Worldly Philosophers</a></i>. In the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Monthly_Review" title="Monthly Review">Monthly Review</a></i> John Bellamy Foster wrote of that journal's founder <a href="/wiki/Paul_Sweezy" title="Paul Sweezy">Paul Sweezy</a>, one of the leading <a href="/wiki/Marxist_economics" class="mw-redirect" title="Marxist economics">Marxist economists</a> in the United States and a graduate assistant of Schumpeter's at Harvard, that Schumpeter "played a formative role in his development as a thinker".<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Other outstanding students of Schumpeter's include the economists <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Georgescu-Roegen" title="Nicholas Georgescu-Roegen">Nicholas Georgescu-Roegen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hyman_Minsky" title="Hyman Minsky">Hyman Minsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Kenneth_Galbraith" title="John Kenneth Galbraith">John Kenneth Galbraith</a> and former chairman of the Federal Reserve, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Greenspan" title="Alan Greenspan">Alan Greenspan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Future Nobel Laureate <a href="/wiki/Robert_Solow" title="Robert Solow">Robert Solow</a> was his student at Harvard, and he expanded on Schumpeter's theory.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, Schumpeter has a following outside standard textbook economics, in areas such as economic policy, management studies, industrial policy, and the study of <a href="/wiki/Innovation" title="Innovation">innovation</a>. Schumpeter was probably the first scholar to develop theories about <a href="/wiki/Entrepreneurship" title="Entrepreneurship">entrepreneurship</a>. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>'s innovation program, and its main development plan, the <a href="/wiki/Lisbon_Strategy" title="Lisbon Strategy">Lisbon Strategy</a>, are influenced by Schumpeter. The <a href="/wiki/International_Joseph_A._Schumpeter_Society" title="International Joseph A. Schumpeter Society">International Joseph A. Schumpeter Society</a> awards the Schumpeter Prize.
</p><p>The Schumpeter <a href="/wiki/Business_school" title="Business school">School of Business and Economics</a> opened in October 2008 at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wuppertal" title="University of Wuppertal">University of Wuppertal</a>, Germany. According to University President Professor Lambert T. Koch, "Schumpeter will not only be the name of the Faculty of Management and Economics, but this is also a research and teaching programme related to Joseph A. Schumpeter."<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 17 September 2009, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> inaugurated a column on business and management named "Schumpeter."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>  The publication has a history of naming columns after significant figures or symbols in the covered field, including naming its British affairs column after former editor <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bagehot" title="Walter Bagehot">Walter Bagehot</a> and its European affairs column after <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>. The initial Schumpeter column praised him as a "champion of innovation and entrepreneurship" whose writing showed an understanding of the benefits and dangers of business that proved to be far ahead of its time.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_works">Major works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Schumpeter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Major works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
